# Recent advances in Machine Learning have transformed numerous industrial sectors, yet classical paradigms face fundamental limitations: rapidly growing data volumes, rising computational costs, significant energy consumption, and the physical scaling limits of conventional hardware architectures. Quantum computing has emerged as a promising computational paradigm to address these challenges, giving rise to the field of Quantum Machine Learning (QML). In this thesis, the theoretical foundations of QML are investigated, with a focus on near-term and future practical applications. Three central challenges are addressed: the trainability of variational quantum circuits, their expressivity, and their resistance to efficient classical simulation. The trainability of Hamming-weight preserving variational quantum circuits is first studied, and theoretical guarantees are established that resolve an open conjecture on the absence of barren plateaus for this circuit family. Subspace-preserving QML algorithms are then introduced, including photonic circuits and quantum convolutional neural networks, and are designed to mimic classical ML subroutines while offering polynomial quantum advantage. Finally, variational quantum circuits are analyzed as quantum Fourier models, and a framework is derived to jointly characterize expressivity and trainability, from which conditions are obtained under which quantum models provably separate from their classical counterparts. These contributions are intended to advance the theoretical roadmap for harnessing near-term and future quantum technologies in real-world applications. ABSTRACT (F RANГҮAIS )

# Les rГ©centes avancГ©es en apprentissage automatique ont transformГ© de nombreux secteurs industriels, mais les paradigmes classiques se heurtent Г  des limites fondamentales : volumes de donnГ©es croissants, coГ»ts de calcul Г©levГ©s, consommation Г©nergГ©tique importante, et contraintes physiques sur le passage Г  lвҖҷГ©chelle des architectures matГ©rielles conventionnelles. LвҖҷinformatique quantique a Г©mergГ© comme un paradigme computationnel prometteur pour dГ©passer ces limites, donnant naissance au domaine de lвҖҷapprentissage au-tomatique quantique (QML). Dans cette thГЁse, les fondements thГ©oriques du QML sont Г©tudiГ©s, avec un accent sur les applications pratiques Г  court et moyen terme. Trois dГ©fis centraux sont abordГ©s : la capacitГ© dвҖҷentraГ®nement des circuits quantiques variationnels, leur expressivitГ©, et leur rГ©sistance Г  aux mГ©thodes de simulation et dвҖҷapproximation classiques. La capacitГ© dвҖҷentraГ®nement des circuits quantiques variationnels prГ©servant le poids de Hamming est tout dвҖҷabord Г©tudiГ©e, et des garanties thГ©oriques sont Г©tablies, rГ©solvant une conjecture ouverte sur lвҖҷabsence de Barren Plateaux pour cette famille de circuits. Des algorithmes QML prГ©servant les sous-espaces sont ensuite introduits, incluant des circuits photoniques et des rГ©seaux de neurones convolutifs quantiques, conГ§us pour reproduire des sous-routines dвҖҷapprentissage classiques tout en offrant un avantage quantique polynomial. Ces contributions visent Г  faire progresser la communautГ© scientifique pour lвҖҷexploitation des technologies quantiques Г  court et moyen terme dans des applications industrielles. # Les rГ©centes avancГ©es en apprentissage automatique ont transformГ© de nombreux secteurs industriels : santГ©, finance, logistique, fabrication, dГ©couverte de matГ©ri-aux, en permettant la modГ©lisation prГ©dictive, lвҖҷoptimisation et lвҖҷautomatisation Г  une Г©chelle sans prГ©cГ©dent. Cependant, le paradigme classique de lвҖҷapprentissage automatique se heurte Г  des limites fondamentales : croissance rapide des volumes de donnГ©es, coГ»ts de calcul croissants, consommation Г©nergГ©tique considГ©rable des grands modГЁles, et limites physiques des architectures matГ©rielles conventionnelles. Ces dГ©fis motivent lвҖҷexploration de nouveaux paradigmes computationnels capables dвҖҷГ©tendre les capacitГ©s de lвҖҷapprentissage automatique classique et de fournir un avantage concurrentiel dans des applications rГ©elles. LвҖҷinformatique quantique a Г©mergГ© comme un candidat prometteur pour dГ©passer ces limites. Bien que Richard Feynman ait initialement imaginГ© son usage dans les annГ©es 1980 pour accГ©lГ©rer la simulation de systГЁmes physiques quantiques, des propositions plus rГ©centes ont dГ©montrГ© des algorithmes promettant des accГ©lГ©rations exponentielles pour des problГЁmes pertinents pour lвҖҷindustrie. Dans le contexte de lвҖҷapprentissage automatique quantique (QML), lвҖҷalgorithme HHL pour la rГ©solution de systГЁmes dвҖҷГ©quations linГ©aires en est un exemple cГ©lГЁbre. Cependant, cet algorithme repose sur des hypothГЁses mathГ©matiques trГЁs restrictives et des exigences matГ©rielles difficiles Г  garantir en pratique, illustrant lвҖҷimportance de lire attentivement les conditions dвҖҷapplication de tels rГ©sultats thГ©oriques. Bien que des algorithmes QML pour ordinateurs quantiques tolГ©rants aux fautes aient Г©tГ© proposГ©s pour plusieurs appli-cations importantes, des efforts considГ©rables restent nГ©cessaires pour concevoir des mГ©thodes compatibles avec le matГ©riel actuel. Outre les avancГ©es technologiques cГҙtГ© hardware nГ©cessaires Г  lвҖҷГ©mergence de lвҖҷusage de lвҖҷinformatique quantique, les mГ©thodes computationnelles proposГ©es sont source de dГ©bat quant Г  leurs capacitГ© Г  dГ©passer les capacitГ© du calcul classique. Des travaux ont montrГ© que des modГЁles classiques peuvent parfois Г©galer les performances dвҖҷalgorithmes quantiques qui ont initialement Г©tГ© dГ©crits comme plus performants. Les algorithmes quantiques variationnels ont Г©mergГ© comme lвҖҷune des approches les plus prometteuses pour le court terme, en employant une approche hybride et des optimiseurs classiques pour entraГ®ner des circuits quantiques paramГ©trГ©s, les rendant naturellement adaptГ©s aux contraintes des dispositifs NISQ. MalgrГ© leur promesse, la communautГ© QML fait face Г  trois dГ©fis majeurs interdГ©pendants : garantir la convergence des mГ©thodes entraГ®nant des circuits quantiques variationnels, contrainte par le phГ©nomГЁne de gradient Г©vanescent dit Barren Plateau ; garantir lвҖҷexpressivitГ©, cвҖҷest-Г -dire donner des garanties fortes quant Г  lвҖҷintГ©ret des mГ©thodes lors dвҖҷun passage Г  lвҖҷГ©chelle, pour laquelle des mГ©triques ont Г©tГ© proposГ©es mais dont une valeur Г©levГ©e conduit souvent Г  des problГЁmes dвҖҷentraГ®nabilitГ© ; et la rГ©sistance Г  la simulation classique efficace, qui conditionne lвҖҷexistence dвҖҷun vГ©ritable avantage quantique. En lвҖҷabsence dвҖҷune recette gГ©nГ©rale permettant de concevoir des mГ©thodes quantiques prov-ablement utiles Г  lвҖҷГ©chelle industrielle, cette thГЁse propose dвҖҷaborder plusieurs de ces dГ©fis thГ©oriques. Le chapitre des prГ©liminaires prГ©sente les notions mathГ©matiques fondamentales nГ©ces-saires Г  la comprГ©hension des rГ©sultats. Une mesure dвҖҷexpressivitГ© couramment utilisГ©e pour un circuit quantique variationnel est la proximitГ© de la distribution des matrices unitaires gГ©nГ©rГ©es Г  la mesure de Haar, caractГ©risГ©e par la notion de 2-design . Sous cette hypothГЁse, le modГЁle quantique prГ©sente un phГ©nomГЁne de concentration exponentielle : sa variance est inversement proportionnelle Г  la dimension de lвҖҷespace mathГ©matique qui dГ©finit le circuit, conduisant aux Barren Plateaux. Les circuits prГ©servant les sous-espaces sont ensuite prГ©sentГ©s, ainsi que leurs propriГ©tГ©s dвҖҷentraГ®nabilitГ© et de simulation classique. En particulier, lвҖҷalgГЁbre de Lie dynamique (DLA) caractГ©rise le groupe de Lie associГ© aux matrices unitaires atteignables : si la DLA est de dimension polynomiale, le circuit peut ГӘtre simulГ© classiquement en temps polynomial. Une conjecture prГ©existante dans la littГ©rature proposait que la variance du gradient de la fonction de coГ»t soit inversement proportionnelle Г  la dimension de la DLA ; cette conjecture est invalidГ©e dans le chapitre suivant. Le troisiГЁme chapitre est consacrГ© Г  lвҖҷГ©tude de lвҖҷentraГ®nabilitГ© et de la contrГҙlabilitГ© de circuits quantiques variationnels prГ©servant le poids de Hamming (HW). Ces circuits utilisent des portes Г  deux qubits, en particulier la porte Reconfigurable Beam Splitter (RBS) et la porte 

Fermionic Beam Splitter (FBS), qui prГ©servent les sous-espaces engendrГ©s par les Г©tats de base Г  poids de Hamming fixГ©. La porte FBS prГ©sente une contrГҙlabilitГ© infГ©rieure Г  la porte RBS : le comportement du circuit dans un sous-espace Г  HW k > 1 est entiГЁrement dГ©terminГ© par celui dans le sous espace HW 1, la dimension maximale de la DLA Г©tant bornГ©e par n(n вҲ’ 1)/2. La notion dвҖҷencodeur de donnГ©es quantique prГ©servant le HW est introduite, rГ©alisant un encodage dвҖҷamplitude dans le sous-espace de HW k. La contrГҙlabilitГ© du circuit est Г©tudiГ©e Г  lвҖҷaide de la Matrice dвҖҷInformation de Fisher Quantique (QFIM), dont le rang maximal est une mГ©trique de contrГҙlabilitГ© dans lвҖҷespace des Г©tats. Deux algorithmes sont proposГ©s pour concevoir de tels encodeurs : lвҖҷun procГЁde par ajout itГ©ratif de portes jusquвҖҷГ  saturation du rang de la QFIM, lвҖҷautre par rГ©duction depuis un circuit surparamГ©trisГ©. Le rГ©sultat central de ce chapitre Г©tablit, sans aucune hypothГЁse de 2 -design, une expression analytique exacte de la variance du gradient de la fonction de coГ»t pour les circuits RBS et FBS. Il est dГ©montrГ© que si les portes sont organisГ©es en une structure pГ©riodique connectГ©e ( Connected Periodic Structure Ansatz ), alors aprГЁs un nombre polynomial de rГ©pГ©titions, la variance du gradient dГ©croГ®t avec la dimension de lвҖҷespace de Hilbert associГ© Г  un HW fixГ©. Ce rГ©sultat dГ©montre lвҖҷabsence de Barren Plateau pour ces circuits lorsque le HW est fixГ©, et invalide la conjecture mentionnГ©e : la variance du gradient dГ©pend de la dimension du sous-espace de Hilbert et non de la dimension de la DLA. Par ailleurs, bien que le rГ©gime oГ№ ces garanties thГ©oriques sont obtenues soit classiquement simulable en temps polynomial, un avantage polynomial reste envisageable, notamment pour des plateformes photoniques Г  haut taux de rГ©pГ©tition. Le quatriГЁme chapitre sвҖҷintГ©resse aux circuits dвҖҷoptique quantique linГ©aire, plateformes particuliГЁrement prometteuses pour le calcul quantique Г  court terme en raison de leur taux de rГ©pГ©tition Г©levГ© et de leurs propriГ©tГ©s naturelles de prГ©servation du nombre de photons. Un rГ©seau optique linГ©aire sur m modes est caractГ©risГ© par lвҖҷhomomorphisme photonique 

П• : SU (m) вҶ’ SU (dn), qui relie lвҖҷunitaire dвҖҷГ©volution Г  un photon W 1 вҲҲ SU (m) Г  lвҖҷunitaire dвҖҷГ©volution Г  n photons W n = П•(W 1) via des permanents de matrices. Dans ce chapitre, une attention particuliГЁre est accordГ©e au contrГҙle de ces circuits photoniques : la dimension de lвҖҷensemble des matrices unitaires accessibles par un circuit dвҖҷoptique linГ©aire est bornГ©e par min (p, m2 вҲ’ 1), oГ№ p est le nombre de portes paramГ©trГ©es. Cette limitation de contrГҙlabilitГ© est une contrainte sur lвҖҷexpressivitГ© du modГЁle de sortie. Pour dГ©passer cette limite, un nouveau schГ©ma dвҖҷ injection dвҖҷГ©tat (SI) est proposГ© : il consiste Г  mesurer un ou plusieurs modes entre deux blocs dвҖҷoptique linГ©aire et Г  rГ©-injecter des photons selon le rГ©sultat de la mesure, sans nГ©cessiter de reconfiguration en temps rГ©el du circuit quantique. Ce schГ©ma se distingue du schГ©ma dвҖҷoptique linГ©aire adaptative par lвҖҷabsence de reconfiguration des unitaires en temps rГ©el et par un dГ©lai optique considГ©rablement rГ©duit dans le cas dвҖҷutilisation de commutateur Г©lectronique ou piezo-Г©lectrique en comparaison Г  la reconfiguration de portes quantiques basГ©s sur des effets thermiques. Est ensuite Г©tudiГ© la puretГ© de lвҖҷГ©tat quantique de sortie aprГЁs plusieurs couches dвҖҷinjection. Des bornes infГ©rieures sont offertes pour lвҖҷГ©volution de cette puretГ© en utilisant diffГ©rentes hypothГЁses, notamment le rГ©gime de non-collision. Le lien entre la puretГ© et la capacitГ© Г  distinguer deux Г©tats de sorties diffГ©rents est prГ©sentГ©, avec la notion introduite de distinguabilitГ© des Г©tats de sortie. Enfin, il est montrГ© que lвҖҷoptique linГ©aire augmentГ©e dвҖҷinjection dвҖҷГ©tat permet de gГ©nГ©rer des probabilitГ©s de sortie qui sont conjecturГ©es comme difficiles Г  calculer classiquement, en Г©chappant aux algorithmes classiques permettant une estimation efficacaes de ces probabilitГ©s. Le cinquiГЁme chapitre prГ©sente de nouveaux algorithmes QML basГ©s sur les circuits prГ©ser-vant les sous-espaces, conГ§us pour remplacer des sous-routines dвҖҷapprentissage automatique classiques tout en offrant un avantage quantique polynomial. Une architecture de rГ©seau de neurones convolutif quantique prГ©servant le poids de Hamming est proposГ©e, analogue au CNN classique : une couche de convolution par filtre RBS, une couche de pooling par portes CNOT et mesures rГ©alisant une non-linГ©aritГ© similaire Г  un average pooling tout en prГ©servant la structure tensorielle de lвҖҷГ©tat, et une couche dense orthogonale rГ©alisГ©e par un rГ©seau de neurones orthog-onal via des portes RBS. Des simulations sur les jeux de donnГ©es MNIST, Fashion-MNIST et CIFAR-10 montrent que lвҖҷarchitecture QCNN atteint des performances comparables au CNN classique avec un nombre rГ©duit de paramГЁtres (755 contre 990), et dГ©passe mГӘme le CNN classique sur MNIST et Fashion-MNIST. Une version photonique de cette architecture exploite lвҖҷencodage tensoriel sur des Г©tats de Fock : une image classique est encodГ©e dans une superposition dвҖҷГ©tats Г  un photon par registre, la couche de convolution est rГ©alisГ©e Г  lвҖҷaide de BeamSplitters, et la couche de pooling est rГ©alisГ©e grГўce au protocole dвҖҷinjection dвҖҷГ©tat prГ©sentГ© dans le chapitre prГ©cГ©dent. Cette architecture a Г©tГ© validГ©e expГ©rimentalement sur une plateforme photonique hybride, comprenant une source de photons uniques Г  boГ®te quantique semi-conductrice, deux circuits intГ©grГ©s programmables Г  8 et 12 modes, et des dГ©tecteurs supraconducteurs Г  nanofils. Les rГ©sultats de classification sur des jeux dвҖҷimages de barres et de rayures ( 4 Г— 4 pixels) et MNIST ( 8 Г— 8 pixels) donnent des prГ©cisions comparables aux rГ©sultats de rГ©fГ©rence pour les architectures QCNN tolГ©rantes aux fautes. Le sixiГЁme chapitre Г©tudie lвҖҷexpressivitГ© des circuits quantiques variationnels Г  travers le prisme des modГЁles de Fourier. Il est Г©tabli que tout circuit variationnel qui encode des donnГ©es classiques en paramГ©trant des hamiltoniens avec ces donnГ©es peut ГӘtre dГ©crit comme une sГ©rie de Fourier dans lвҖҷentrГ©e classique : f (x, Оё ) = вҲ‘ПүвҲҲО© cПү (Оё )eiПүT x, oГ№ le spectre О© est dГ©terminГ© par les valeurs propres des hamiltoniens dвҖҷencodage et les coefficients de Fourier dГ©pendent principalement des couches entraГ®nables. Une notion cruciale est introduite : la redondance 

|R(Пү)| dвҖҷune frГ©quence Пү, dГ©finie comme le nombre de paires de chemins dans lвҖҷarbre spectral quantique gГ©nГ©rant cette frГ©quence. Sous lвҖҷhypothГЁse que chaque couche entraГ®nable forme un 2 -design exact, la variance du coefficient de Fourier cПү est proportionnelle Г  la redondance normalisГ©e ^|R(Пү)| := |R(Пү)|/d2 : les frГ©quences Г  haute redondance prГ©sentent une variance relativement plus grande, tandis que celles Г  faible redondance sont exponentiellement concen-trГ©es. Ce rГ©sultat est Г©tendu au cas de lвҖҷhypothГЁse dвҖҷapproximate 2 -design : la variance reste bornГ©e par un polynГҙme en ^|R(Пү)| et en Оө, dГ©montrant que le phГ©nomГЁne dвҖҷexpressivitГ© Г©vanes-cente, situation oГ№ la variance de certains coefficients de Fourier dГ©croГ®t exponentiellement, peut persister au-delГ  de lвҖҷhypothГЁse exacte de 2-design. Le cas dвҖҷune architecture constituГ©e de blocs locaux est Г©galement traitГ©, permettant de capturer lвҖҷinteraction entre un observable local et le circuit. Il est dГ©montrГ© que lвҖҷexpressivitГ© Г©vanescente est conceptuellement distincte de la concentration du modГЁle entier : des rГ©gimes existent oГ№ les coefficients de Fourier sont expo-nentiellement concentrГ©s sans que le modГЁle global le soit. pour tout paramГЁtre Оё . Des simulations numГ©riques confirment que les frГ©quences Г  faible redondance sont plus difficiles Г  atteindre lors de lвҖҷentraГ®nement, Г©tayant lвҖҷintuition que lвҖҷexpressivitГ© Г©vanescente affecte la convergence pratique du modГЁle. Le septiГЁme chapitre Г©tudie les conditions sous lesquelles les modГЁles QML peuvent Г©viter lвҖҷapproximation classique basГ©e sur une mГ©thode de machine learning classique appelГ©e "Ran-dom Fourier Features". Les VQCs sont des modГЁles linГ©aires dans un espace de caractГ©ristiques : 

fQ(x) = ОІ вҠӨ 

> Q

ПҶ (x). Dans le rГ©gime surparamГ©trГ© ( p > M), la descente de gradient classique con-verge vers lвҖҷestimateur de norme minimale ( Minimum Norm Least Squares , MNLS) parmi les solutions qui minimisent le risque empirique. Contrairement au cas classique, le vecteur de poids dвҖҷun VQC ne reste pas dans lвҖҷespace engendrГ© par les donnГ©es dвҖҷentraГ®nement et ne converge pas vers le MNLS ; cвҖҷest cette distinction qui constitue la condition nГ©cessaire pour un avantage quantique. Un modГЁle quantique interpolant fQ prГ©sente un avantage quantique po-tentiel si son vecteur de poids est de norme trГЁs supГ©rieure Г  celle du MNLS. Cette condition est analysГ©e pour plusieurs cas dвҖҷГ©tude, dans le cadre de donnГ©es continues comme discrГЁtes. Sont notamment considГ©rГ©s des modГЁles quantiques associГ©es Г  des fonctions difficiles Г  rГ©aliser clas-siquement et dвҖҷimportance pour des problГЁmes de cryptographie, comme le logarithme discret. La dequantisation est impossible en cohГ©rence avec la complexitГ© supposГ©e du problГЁme. Les liens entre la norme du vecteur de poids et la concentration du modГЁle sont Г©galement discutГ©s. Les exemples dГ©veloppГ©s avec des entrГ©es continues rГ©alisent la condition nГ©cessaire pour Г©viter la mГ©thode dвҖҷapproximation classique, mais au prix dвҖҷune concentration des modГЁles qui empГӘche leurs correctes entraГ®nements. Un rГ©sultat constructif Г©tablit lвҖҷexistence de familles de modГЁles de Fourier avec un grand vecteur de poids, non concentrГ©s et bornГ©s, dont la rГ©alisation par un circuit quantique constitue une question ouverte centrale. Cette thГЁse adopte une approche pragmatique face au paradoxe fondamental du QML : les rГ©gimes oГ№ des garanties thГ©oriques rigoureuses peuvent ГӘtre obtenues (absence de Barren Plateau, entraГ®nabilitГ© prouvГ©e) sont souvent classiquement simulables en temps polynomial, remettant en cause lвҖҷavantage exponentiel initialement recherchГ©. PlutГҙt que de viser un avantage exponentiel, la thГЁse sвҖҷintГ©resse Г  exploiter un avantage polynomial important, com-binГ© Г  un matГ©riel appropriГ© comme des plateformes photoniques Г  haut taux de rГ©pГ©tition, afin de rendre lвҖҷinformatique quantique pratiquement utile, en particulier pour accГ©lГ©rer des blocs de construction fondamentaux de lвҖҷintelligence artificielle. Ces travaux, majoritairement thГ©oriques, contribuent Г  faire progresser la communautГ© scientifique pour lвҖҷexploitation des technologies quantiques Г  court et moyen terme dans des applications industrielles rГ©elles. # 1

# INTRODUCTION 

# 1.1 QML for Industrial Applications 

Over the past decades, advances in Machine Learning (ML) have transformed a wide range of industries, enabling predictive modeling, optimization, and automation at unprecedented scales. From healthcare and finance to logistics, manufacturing, and materials discovery, data-driven methods are increasingly central to decision-making and innovation. However, the classical paradigm of ML faces fundamental challenges that vary depending on the use case: the rapid growth of data volumes, the rising computational cost of training complex models, concerns over security and privacy, the significant energy consumption of large-scale ML models, and the physical limits of conventional hardware architectures in scaling to ever larger problem sizes. These challenges motivate the exploration of new computational paradigms that can extend the capabilities of classical ML and provide a competitive advantage in real-world applications. Quantum computing has emerged as a promising candidate to address these challenges. While Richard Feynman originally advocated its use in the 1980s for accelerating the simulation of quantum physics and chemistry, more recent proposals have demonstrated algorithms that promise exponential speedups over classical methods for problems highly relevant to industry. In the context of Quantum Machine Learning (QML), one of the most famous examples is the HHL algorithm (named after Aram Harrow, Avinatan Hassidim, and Seth Lloyd) for solving systems of linear equations in logarithmic time [ 1 ]. However, this algorithm relies on stringent resource requirements and mathematical assumptions that are difficult to guarantee in practice, highlighting the importance of carefully reading the fine print of such theoretical results [ 2 ]. 1CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION 

Although QML algorithms for fault-tolerant quantum computers (FTQCs) have been proposed for several impactful applications [ 3, 4 , 5 ], significant effort is still required to design methods that are compatible with near-term hardware [ 6 ]. In addition with strong hardware requirements, the community is still proposing new methods, and debating of the impact of existing proposals. This debate on "dequantization" of quantum methods can be illustrated by [ 7 ], where the author proposed a classical model that matches the performance of previously proposed quantum algorithms for principal component analysis [ 3 ] and nearest-centroid clustering [ 8]. These efforts by the community are crucial not only to enable the early adoption of quantum computers in industry, but also to support hardware providers by demonstrating applications that require fewer resources. Variational quantum algorithms (VQAs) [ 9 ] have emerged as one of the most promising approaches for developing near-term useful methods. These algorithms employ classical opti-mizers to train parametrized quantum circuits, making them naturally suited to the constraints of noisy intermediate-scale quantum (NISQ) devices. Despite their promise, the integration of VQAs into industry still faces significant challenges, including issues of trainability and the difficulty of rigorously identifying and formalizing quantum advantage. As a result, directly mapping existing VQA-based methods to industrial use cases is, at present, premature. Instead, the community must focus on developing new algorithms and theoretical tools that can bridge the gap between todayвҖҷs hardware and the demands of industrial utility. This thesis investigates the intersection of quantum machine learning and its potential for practical applications, with a focus on theoretical foundations and mathematical analysis. As there is currently no general recipe to design quantum methods that are provably useful at scale for solving industrial problem, this thesis propose to address several of the theoretical challenges that have emerged in recent years. In particular, it explores what forms of plausible quantum advantage can be expected, identifies theoretical conditions under which algorithmic guarantees may be established, and characterizes regimes in which quantum models resist efficient classical simulation. Through these contributions, this work aims to advance the road-map for harnessing near-term and future quantum technologies for practical impact. 

# 1.2 Objectives and QML Challenges 

Machine Learning has made it possible to solve many industrial problems that traditional computational methods either struggled with or could only address at a higher cost. Intuitively, quantum computing seems like a good candidate to improve some machine learning methods, 21.2. OBJECTIVES AND QML CHALLENGES No Surrogate 

Model 

Training Expressivity 

> Fourier Models & Random Fourier Features
> (Chapter 7)
> Focus on HW Preserving
> Circuits (Chapter 3)

> HW Preserving (Chapter 3)

> Subspace Preserving Algorithms (Chapter 5)

Fig. 1.1: Quantum Machine Learning Challenges, and corresponding part of the Thesis. as it offers large computational speed up on some problems. In particular, the output model of variational quantum circuits can be considered as a linear algebraic computation in a very large Hilbert space. However, the Quantum Machine Learning (QML) community is facing important challenges in the design of useful near-term applications. First, training unitary matrices of exponential size seems to cause a vanishing gradient phenomena called Barren Plateau [ 10 , 11 ], limiting the 

trainability of variational quantum circuits. Secondly, the QML community has tried to offer figures of merit to characterize the quality of quantum models, that can be called expressivity .While the pertinence of such figures of merit will be discussed in this Thesis, it has been shown that having high expressivity metrics often leads to trainability issues. Finally, studies have shown that approximating or simulating quantum circuits, and quantum models can sometime be done using surrogate models .In this Thesis, each of theses challenges will be discussed and compared to the recent advancements of the community. First, the trainability of subspace preserving variational quantum circuits is studied, as those circuits are believed to avoid Barren Plateau under an uniform distribution hypothesis (discussed in chapter 2) over a particular group of interest. In chapter 3, a focus on Hamming Weight (HW) preserving circuits is presented, with theoretical guarantees on the trainability of such circuits with a different set of hypothesis, answering a prior conjecture on the trainability of such circuit. As with other studies from the community, these results tend to show that regimes where theoretical guarantees on the training can be offered also turn out to be "classically simulable" regimes, i.e., cases wher the computation can 3CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION 

be done on a classical computer in polynomial time. In later part of the thesis, such regime is handled as polynomial advantage can still be valuable for quantum industrial utility, especially with high repetition rate such as photonic ones. Figures of merit for expressivity are discussed in the Thesis. In particular, the controllability of quantum circuits is studied in chapter 3 and chapter 4, and motivation for its importance is presented through the lens of the need for a quantum data loading procedure. However, theoretical figures of merit are not always convincing to ensure the utility of a quantum algorithm for industrial scales. In chapter 5, the choice of mimicking classical machine learning subroutines is made, in order to motivate the good scaling of the newly designed algorithms. Finally, the expressivity of variational quantum circuits is studied through the lens of Fourier quantum models in chapter 6. This framework, not restricted to subspace preserving quantum circuits, is quite general and allows to compare quantum circuit outputs and classical learning methods. In particular, it allows to compare their convergence, and to discuss the idea of approximation techniques as developed in chapter 7. 

# 1.3 Thesis Outline 

In chapter 2, important mathematical notions, tools, and frameworks are introduced. Figures of merit and important concepts for expressivity, trainability, classical simulation and approx-imation that are useful for each part of the thesis are presented. In particular, the theory of subspace preserving variational quantum circuits is explored as an introductive part both of Hamming-weight preserving quantum circuits and photonic algorithms later discussed. This chapter recalls the state of the art results and explains why the following studies contributed to the quantum machine learning field, and to the theoretical development of applications. In chapter 3, a study of specific Hamming weight preserving variational quantum circuits is proposed. In particular, the control of such circuits is studied through the lens of the design of a quantum data loader, and theoretical results on its trainability are offered and compared with recent results. In chapter 4, a study of linear optical quantum circuits, that are near term, suboptimal, and particle number preserving is proposed. Limitations on the controllability capacities are highlighted, and a new scheme for near term applications is offered. 41.4. CONTRIBUTIONS 

In chapter 5, new quantum machine learning building blocks are presented, using Hamming weight preserving circuits, particle number preserving circuits, and the scheme previously introduced. This chapter offers a change in perspective: using a suboptimal model to offer a polynomial advantage while mimicking classical machine learning building blocks to ensure utility, along with theoretical guarantees on the training and good scaling of the performances. In chapter 6, variational quantum circuits are studied as Fourier models. Expressivity and its connections with trainability are explored, offering theoretical tools to design such circuits. In chapter 7, the study of variational quantum circuits Fourier models is used to design surrogate models and to compare the convergence of classical and quantum models. Such results allow for guidelines to ensure a separation between quantum and classical learning models.

# 2

# PRELIMINARIES 

# This chapter presents the mathematical notions that are important to understand the re-sults of the Thesis the status of the QML community at the time that these projects were carried out. Basic knowledge of linear algebra, quantum computing, and variational quantum circuits is assumed. First, the main metric of expressivity for quantum models used by the community is introduced, which corresponds to studying the distribution of achievable unitary matrices. Importantly, a distribution that is uniform over an exponentially large group is generally interpreted as highly expressive and enables the use of powerful mathematical tools such as the Weingarten calculus. These tools were used to show that a uniform distribution over an exponentially large group implies a link between model concentration and expressivity. This can lead to vanishing gradient phenomena during the training of quantum circuits, an important problem known as the Barren Plateau (BP) phenomenon, which is recalled here. Subspace-preserving variational quantum circuits are also introduced, along with existing results from the literature on their trainability and classical simulation. A prior conjecture on trainability is recalled, which is later invalidated in chapter 3. These results help to understand what is often referred to as a curse of dimensionality in variational quantum circuits. The general intuition supported by this Thesis and other works is that circuits that are exponentially hard to simulate classically also tend to exhibit Barren Plateaus, at least in settings where theoretical guarantees can be provided. This Thesis focuses on the use of subspace-preserving quantum circuits acting on polynomial-sized subspaces, aiming for quantum utility based on a polynomial advantage. This is particularly relevant for suboptimal photonic hardware (based on linear optics and adaptivity techniques as introduced in chapter 4) that offers a high 

9CHAPTER 2. PRELIMINARIES 

repetition rate. In addition, other figures of merit for expressivity are discussed, such as the controllability of quantum circuits and their corresponding Fourier models. 

# 2.1 Expressivity Measures of Quantum Models 

This Section discusses the different metrics of expressivity that are relevant to this study. 

## 2.1.1 Distribution of Trainable Unitary Matrices and Model Concentration 

A common expressivity measure for a variational quantum circuit (VQC) that has been exten-sively used in the literature [ 19 , 20 ] is how uniformly the ensemble of the generated unitaries explores the unitary group. When learning a function generated by a VQC, it is usually suffi-cient to characterize the distance to the Haar measure up to the second moment. Hence, a VQC that forms a 2-design is defined as follows: 

Definition 2.1 (2-design) . A VQC U(Оҳ) is said to form a 2-design if the ensemble of unitaries it generates {U(Оё )}Оё вҲҲОҳ (understood with a chosen distribution on the parameter space Оҳ)produces first and second moments equal to those of the Haar measure over the space of all unitaries. The 2nd moment superoperator of the distribution generated by U (Оҳ) is defined as: M(2)

## 2.1.2 Fourier Expressivity of Quantum Models 

Along with the expressivity characterization of the parameterized part in a quantum model by its Оө-distance to a 2-design, the expressivity of a quantum model can also be examined through its Fourier representation, i.e. the signature of the specific Hamiltonian encoding strategy. In chapter 6 and chapter 7, we study those quantum models in the setting where we can express them as Fourier models. In a recent work [ 25 ], authors have proposed to define the 

Fourier expressivity as the smallest set of functions such that the quantum model defined in Eq. (2.3) could be expressed as a linear combination of those functions. According to this definition and the Fourier decomposition of the quantum model, the Fourier expressivity is bounded by the spectrum size. In chapter 6, the focus is on characterizing the expressivity of a Quantum model through Fourier lens. Indeed, it is explained how individual Fourier coefficients may suffer from exponential concentration depending on the spectrum distribution. Therefore, the term van-ishing expressivity is introduced, to describe cases where some or all Fourier coefficients are exponentially concentrated around their mean. 

# 2.2 Susbpace Preserving Variational Quantum Circuits 

This Section presents results and definitions for subspace preserving quantum circuits that were introduced by the QML community before or during the writing of the Thesis. Those results offer perspectives on the studies conducted in the following chapters, where subspace preserving methods are deployed for polynomial advantage and to avoid BPs, especially for non-universal photonic devices. 12 2.2. ## 2.2.1 Subspace Preserving VQCs and Lie Algebra 

First, a particular case of interest for subspace preserving variational quantum circuits is the Lie Algebraic Supported Ansatz defined in [ 26 ]. By choosing a specific set of Hamiltonian matrices to define a variational quantum circuit, its action can be limited to a set of unitary matrices of smaller dimension, called a Lie Group. 

2.2.1.1 Formal Definitions 

A general formulation of a parametrized unitary matrix is given by:

g = span вҹЁiH 0, . . . , iH K вҹ©Lie вҠҶ su (d) , (2.10)

2.2.1.2 Lie Algebra Supported Ansatz 

If the generators share common symmetries, i.e., there exists a hermitian operator that commutes with every generator, then every element in g is block diagonal in the eigenbasis of the operator. As a consequence, every H вҲҲ g and every U вҲҲ eig are block diagonal in this particular basis that represents the symmetry. The state space can thus be considered as a direct sum of subspaces that are invariant under the action of g.In this Thesis, two particular symmetries that can be used to design Lie algebra supported ansatz are discussed: вҖў Hamming weight preserving operators in chapter 3, where the Hamming weight (number of qubits in state |1вҹ©) is unchanged during the computation; вҖў Particle number preserving operators in chapter 4, where the number of photons is unchanged. 14 2.2. SUSBPACE PRESERVING VARIATIONAL QUANTUM CIRCUITS 

In the case where the dynamical Lie algebra is a direct sum g = L 

> k

gk, the Hilbert space can be decomposed as well into subspaces H = L 

> k

Hk, with each Hk invariant under the action of the dynamical Lie Group eig. Then, if the initial state ПҒk is supported entirely in igk, only the subspace Hk is of interest during the computation. If the subspace is of polynomial size with respect to the number of qubits, then one can classically simulate every computation in a polynomial time [27]. In [ 24 ], it was shown that such ansatz can be used to avoid Barren Plateaus in VQCs. Similar to [ 19 ], their proof is based on several hypothesis. First, the authors consider the case of subspaces preserved by the action of the Lie group. They then assume that within a subspace 

Hk of dimension dk, the distribution of unitary matrices Uk forms a 2-design in Hk. This requires the Lie algebra to be sufficiently large on Hk, meaning gk = su (dk) or gk = u(dk). As a result, they show that the variance of the cost scales inversely with dk, demonstrating that a polynomial-sized subspace can avoid Barren Plateaus. However, this setting is classically simulable in polynomial time as explained earlier. One important question that remains from this work is whether the variance of the gradient depends on the dimension of the subspace Hk or on the dimension of the Lie algebra gk. A conjecture asserting the dependence on the dimension of the Lie algebra was proposed in [ 24 ]. 

Conjecture 2.1 (From Conjecture 1 in [ 24 ]) . Let the state ПҒ belong to a subspace Hk associated with a subspace dynamical Lie algebra gk (or sub-DLA the subrepresentation in g where ПҒ has support on). Then, the scaling of the variance of the cost function partial derivative is inversely proportional to the scaling of the dimension of the DLA, i.e. Var [вҲӮОјC(Оё )] вҲҲ O

A counterexample is presented in Section 3.3 using a Hamming weight preserving ansatz. In this example, where the initial and target states are normalized real superpositions of states with a fixed Hamming weight and the cost function is the squared Euclidean distance between the output and target states, the variance of the cost gradient depends on the dimension of the subspace Hk.During the writing of this thesis, it was shown in [26, 28] that:

2.2.1.3 Classical Simulation of Subspace Preserving Quantum Circuits 

Quantum circuits that preserve symmetries during computation may offer advantages for classical simulation. As previously stated, if the initial state ПҒk is supported only on the subspace Hk, then this will be the only subspace of interest throughout the computation. If the subspace is of polynomial size with respect to the number of qubits, then every computation within it can be classically simulated in polynomial time. If the subspace is of exponential size but the Lie algebra is of polynomial dimension, then according to Theorem 2.1, Barren Plateaus can be avoided provided the initial state or the observable lie in the Lie algebra. However, it was shown in [ 27 ] that in such a setting, the circuit can be simulated classically in polynomial time using a basis of the Lie algebra 2.  

> 2Note that obtaining such a Lie algebra basis is non-trivial in general, even if the basis is of polynomial size. A general algorithm for retrieving a dynamical Lie algebra basis from a set of Hamiltonian generators is provided in the supplementary materials of [ 12 ]; its time complexity is polynomial in the size of the HamiltonianвҖҷs subspace and the dimension of the Lie algebra.

16 C H A P T E R 

# 3

# HAMMING -W EIGHT PRESERVING QUANTUM CIRCUITS FOR MACHINE LEARNING 

# We previously discussed some properties of subspace preserving quantum circuits that tend to indicate a trade-off between the expressivity and trainability of such methods, in the sense that circuits which avoid Barren Plateaus due to a polynomially large Lie algebra can be simulated in polynomial time. However, those results were established under certain mathematical hypotheses that can be restrictive and were partially published during the writing of this Thesis. In this context, this chapter is dedicated to the study of the trainability and controllability of specific Hamming weight (HW) preserving variational quantum circuits. These circuits use qubit gates that preserve subspaces of the Hilbert space, spanned by basis states with fixed Hamming weight. In this study, the role of controllability is highlighted through the lens of quantum data loading. The trainability of such circuits is explored, not using any 2-design hypothesis but rather arguments on stochastic matrices, and highlights a setting where conjecture 2.1 on the link between controllability and trainability of VQCs does not apply. 

# 3.1 Hamming Weight Preserving Gates: RBS and FBS 

This section presents the most commonly used gates in HW-preserving quantum circuits. First, the Reconfigurable Beam Splitter (RBS) gate is presented, which is easy to implement or native on many quantum devices. It is widely used in the quantum machine learning community to design variational algorithms [ 29 , 30 , 31 , 32 , 33 ] and quantum inspired classical methods [ 34 ]. 17 CHAPTER 3. Although similar to the RBS gate, the FBS is less controllable. Each block Uk for k > 1is completely determined by U1, as it is the k-compound matrix of U1 (see Definition 3.4). Therefore, the controllability of the FBS unitary matrices is upper-bounded by the controllability of the first block that represents the effect of the gate in the subspace of unary states, i.e., states of HW 1. The maximal dimension of the DLA for the FBS is n(n вҲ’ 1)/2. The limitation in its controllability is illustrated in Figure 3.2, and prevents the use of FBS for amplitude encoding on a subspace of HW k with k > 1 . However, FBS can be used to perform Clifford loader [ 35 ], a loader determined on the entire Hilbert space and restricted to the direct sum of subspace produce by the FBS. In [ 35 ], algorithms for quantum determinant sampling, singular value estimation for compound matrices, and for topological data analysis are presented using FBS gates and Clifford loaders. 19 CHAPTER 3. # 3.2 Space-Efficient Amplitude Encoding 

This section discusses the design of HW-preserving quantum data loaders, which illustrates the importance of controllability. First, Amplitude Encoding and HW-preserving quantum data loaders are defined; these concepts will be refined in chapter 5. Then, a method to achieve a data loader efficiently using HW-preserving gates and their subspace-preserving properties is proposed. Since such data loaders are classically simulable, the potential quantum speed-up for training is limited to a polynomial advantage. Nevertheless, the controllability of HW-preserving quantum circuit, analyzed through their DLA dimension and Quantum Fisher Information Matrix (QFIM) rank, is of independent interest. 

## 3.2.1 Hamming Weight Preserving Quantum Data Loaders 

This study starts with the definition of an Amplitude Encoding scheme and HW-preserving quantum data loaders. 

Definition 3.5 (Amplitude Encoding) . An amplitude encoding data loader is a parametrized n -qubit quantum circuit that, given a classical vector x = ( x1, . . . , xd ) вҲҲ Rd (of a certain fixed length d вүӨ 2n), prepares the quantum state:

One could use the Reconfigurable Beam Splitter (RBS) gate to perform such an encoding on the basis of fixed HW (see Definition 3.3). This HW-preserving gate is easy to implement 20 3.2. SPACE-EFFICIENT AMPLITUDE ENCODING 

or native on many quantum devices. Note that the results in this chapter also hold for another HW-preserving gate named the Fermionic Beam Splitter (FBS), which was used for QML applications in [ 35 ] but has less favorable properties in terms of controllability, as explained previously. The data loading scheme is explained as follows. First, the quantum state must be initialized to |esвҹ©, a basis state of HW k. This state is then split onto the states in Bnk using RBS gates. Namely, this cost function does not induce Barren Plateaus even though it is a type of global cost function [36 ], as the Hilbert space corresponding to states of HW k is not exponentially large for small 

k. Subspace preserving quantum circuits are easier to simulate in small subspaces than random quantum circuits over the entire Hilbert space [ 37 ]. In the case of a HW-preserving VQC, the speedup of using a quantum computer grows exponentially with k. Classical simulability of the encoding part itself is not an issue, if it is later combined with a trainable layer that is hard to simulate 1.  

> 1Simple examples can be constructed in which a quantum circuit is first composed of a state preparation layer with a classically simulable encoding layer, followed by a trainable layer that does not respect the same symmetry. For example, one can use a unary encoding layer followed by a circuit made of Xrotation gates and RBS gates. The classical data would be naturally encoded in the unary basis, while the final state does not belong to the same basis.

## 3.2.2 Finding the Quantum Data Loader 

As explained earlier, the DLA gives an upper-bound on the controlability of a variational quantum circuit in the unitary space. Having a DLA dimension high enough is a necessary condition to design a quantum data loader. A remaining question is how to design the quantum data loader from a given subspace and the connectivity that induces the existence of such a circuit. This Section presents two algorithms to design the quantum data loader based on the study of controllability in the state space. Unitary Space State Space 

Fig. 3.3: Representation of the unitary and output state spaces. The DLA is the tangent space of the unitary space. The possible directions for the evolution of the output state are given by the Quantum Fisher Information Matrix eigenvectors. The ability of a subspace-preserving circuit to achieve amplitude encoding on one of its preserved subspaces is equivalent to the circuit perfectly controlling the state space created by its output. In particular, an RBS based VQC would achieve perfect amplitude encoding (see Definition 3.5) on the subspace of HW k if its output state could be any (real, normalized) superposition of states in Bnk . That is, if the space of kets that its output explores were the entirety of a certain sphere of (real) dimension dk вҲ’ 1, noted SdkвҲ’1 and illustrated in Figure 3.3. An essential tool for studying the controllability of a quantum circuit in the state space is defined below. 

Definition 3.6 (Quantum Fisher Information Matrix) . The Quantum Fisher Information Matrix (QFIM) associated to any parametrized pure state |ПҲ(Оё )вҹ© that uses p continuous parameters 

Оё = ( Оё1, . . . , Оёp), is the following p Г— p real matrix assigned to each parameter vector Оё :

Theorem 3.1 (Almost-constant property of QFIM rank) . A VQCвҖҷs output state |ПҲ(Оё )вҹ© always has the following property: almost everywhere on the considered parameter space Оҳ, the rank of their QFIM is constant, equal to rmax := max

Оё вҲҲ Оҳ uniformly at random and calculating its QFIM rank value r(Оё ) yields r(Оё ) = rmax with probability 1.

23 CHAPTER 3. HW PRESERVING METHODS 

Proof. This follows from the fact that the Jacobians of analytic maps have constant rank almost everywhere [ 40 , Prop. B.4], combined with the fact that the QFIM is the Gram matrix of the JacobianвҖҷs columns (for a certain inner product). It would be easy to find specific points in the parameter space where the QFIM rank changes, for example at the poles of the Bloch sphere when considering Pauli rotation gates on a single-qubit state. In Theorem 3.1, "almost everywhere" means with probability one when a parameter is sampled from the uniform distribution on the parameter space. Using the QFIM on a given subspace of HW k, a first algorithm is proposed to design a quantum data loader in this subspace from an initial state, created using bit-flips, and the possible generators G given by the qubit connectivity and the RBS gate Hamiltonian. Reaching the maximal rank (over parameter space) of the QFIM of a quantum data loader circuit is equal to dim (SdkвҲ’1) = dk вҲ’ 1 , is taken as evidence that it may achieve any state in SdkвҲ’1, i.e., achieve the amplitude encoding on the subspace of HW k. Following this idea, Algorithm 1 creates iteratively a circuit by adding RBS gates one at a time, while making sure that each added gate has actually incremented the QFIM rank, and only stops when the QFIM rank attains the dimension of the sphere SdkвҲ’1, suggesting that the data loader capability has been obtained.

Algorithm 2 must be initialized by considering a quantum circuit made of a large number of gates, as determined by the dimension of its DLA in Eq. (3.10) . Those gates can be chosen randomly or in such a way to reduce the circuit depth. Using Algorithm 2 allows to first design a circuit with a number of gate slightly larger than the optimal, and then to reduce the circuit by removing some gates according to the QFIM. This method can be useful to first choose a circuit that corresponds to other figure of merits (favoring the use of qubits of better quality, reducing the depth) and then to avoid to derive too many time the rank of the QFIM which can be costly. In practice, for both algorithms, particular attention must be paid to the order in which generators are tested, with regard to the circuit depth. The computational cost of both algorithms depends on the cost of calculating the rank of the QFIM, which has a computational complexity of O(p2 d2 

> k

+ p3) for classical simulation,where p is the number of parameters and dk is the dimension of the chosen subspace (the p3 term corresponds to the complexity of calculating the matrix rank). Algorithm 1 and Algorithm 2 are thus efficient to run for small subspaces, which correspond to the case where HW-preserving quantum circuits are trainable (see the following Section). Since such data loaders are classically simulable, the potential quantum speed-up for training is limited to a polynomial advantage. However, one could train them 25 CHAPTER 3. HW PRESERVING METHODS 

classically to represent classical data and then associate them with a quantum circuit that is harder to simulateвҖ”for example, by increasing the number of qubits, the Hamming weight, or incorporating gates that do not preserve Hamming weight. 

# 3.3 Trainability of HW-Preserving Quantum Circuits 

It is known that some QML proposals suffer from unfavorable optimization landscape properties [ 10 ] that lead to strong limitations in their trainability. This Section presents strong results on the gradient of the cost function for VQCs composed of RBS or FBS gates. These results are not based on a Haar-random distribution of the unitary matrices, such as the 2-design hypothesis discussed in Section 2.1. First, the backpropagation formalism applied to RBS and FBS based VQCs is presented in Section 3.3.1.Then, the resulting theorems on the variance and expectation value of the cost function gradient are presented in Section 3.3.2. 

## 3.3.1 Backpropagation for Gradient Calculus 

A HW-preserving quantum circuit composed solely of RBS gates or solely of FBS gates is described. We decompose the quantum circuit as a series of such gates, for which we denote their unitary matrices in the basis Bnk by wО» (ОёО» ), for О» = 1, . . . , О»max , with ОёО» denoting the a gateвҖҷs angle parameter. In the subspace of HW k, we denote respectively the initial, intermediate, and final quantum states by О¶ 0, О¶ О» (for О» = 1, . . . , О»max ), and z вҖ” they are all normalized vectors in Rdk . We also denote the inner error associated to the state О» by Оҙ О» := вҲӮC/вҲӮО¶ О» . The cost function we consider in this work is the squared Euclidean distance between the output state z of the circuit and fixed target output y:

C(Оё ) = || z(Оё ) вҲ’ y|| 22 . (3.11) We focus on this cost function because it is ubiquitous in classical machine learning tasks (where it is usually termed the l2 loss ). The reader might notice that the cost function in Equation (3.11) depends on the phase on the output state z (i.e. changing z to вҲ’z generally changes the cost function value), and thus it may not be written as an expectation value вҹЁz| O |zвҹ©

of some hermitian O (as those latter functions are phase invariant). While this is true, it is possible to extend the system with a single ancillary qubit such that C(Оё ) may be estimated through a quantum observable on the extended system (see the second tomography procedure described in [42] for more details). 2

## 3.3.2 Avoiding Barren Plateaus 

The analytic definition of the cost function gradient provided by backpropagation (Equa-tion (3.13) and Equation (3.14)) can be used to study the phenomenon of Barren Plateaus 

(BPs) defined in Definition 2.3, a detrimental situation in which cost function gradients are exponentially suppressed. It is possible to determine the existence of BPs under the assumption that the ensemble of parametrized unitary matrices forms an approximate 2-design [ 19 ]. In that case the quantity Var Оё [вҲӮОёО» C(Оё )] may be evaluated using the standard Weingarten calculus integration formulas (see e.g. [ 21 ]) and found to be inversely proportional to the dimension of the Hilbert space. In [ 24 ], it was shown that if a subspace-preserving VQC satisfies the assumption of full controllability of the subspace (meaning the dimension of the DLA is maximal, i.e., equal to the dimension of all unitary matrices on that subspace), as well as a 2-design assumption on that subspace, then the variance of the cost gradient scales inversely with the dimension of the subspace. As a result, one could avoid BPs using a subspace invariant quantum circuit with a subspace of small dimension. Here, it is shown that BPs can indeed be avoided for subspace-invariant quantum circuits based on RBS or FBS gates, without making a 2-design assumption or any assumption on controllability, provided the circuit is employed only in a given HW k subspace with fixed k

and under certain assumptions on the qubit connectivity. The central result which enables this claim is the following Lemma 3.1, which by leveraging the specific form of RBS/FBS circuits, provides an exact analytic expression for the the variance of the cost gradient (given an initial state and a target state), for our cost function of interest. From this result, it is then possible to prove the absence of BP results depending on the situation of interest. We propose two such applications in that regard, presented as Theorem 3.2 and Theorem 3.3, that deal respectively with the case of a periodic connected ansatz with any input/target states, and an arbitrary circuit with randomly sampled input/target states accordng to a family of distributions. 

Lemma 3.1 (Variance of RBS and FBS based VQCs) . Let us consider an n -qubit HW-preserving VQC made of D вүҘ 1 RBS or FBS gates only, that is employed in the subspace of HW k (i.e. both the initial state О¶ 0 and the target state y are normalized real superpositions of the basis Bnk ), along with the cost function C(Оё ) taken as the squared Euclidean distance between the final state О¶ О»max and the target state y. If Оё is distributed uniformly in Оё := [ 0, 2ПҖ]D,

The fact that this statement concerns gates located at constant ratios of the circuit depth may be interpreted as the condition that the gates are not too close to either extremities of the circuit. We concede that the proof of this theorem depends on the validity of a small conjecture that we make about spectral gaps of certain stochastic matrices (conjecture A.1), for which we present numerical evidence in Section A.2.9. Since Theorem 3.2 states a requirement of a polynomial number of repetitions L to reach its conclusion of absence of Barren Plateaus, one may wonder if it can generally be subsumed by the usual approximate 2-design argument, since usually polynomial repetitions produce approximate 2 -designs (see Theorem 1 in [ 24 ]). A discussion on the subtilities behind this is given in Section A.2.10. The next theorem does not require any lower limit on the circuit depth:

It can be concluded from these results that Barren Plateaus are absent for subspace-invariant RBS and FBS based quantum circuits for a fixed subspace k. It is emphasized that, unlike recent related works [ 24 , 28 , 26 ] (which apply to quite general circuits), Theorem 3.2 for RBS/FBS circuits does not rely on a 2-design assumption for the global unitary of the circuit. # 4

# PHOTONIC SUB -O PTIMAL MODELS 

# Although quantum computers promise large advantages over classical computing, fault-tolerant universal quantum computers are still far from being available. In particular, while photonics is one of the promising platforms for quantum computing, the technological requirements for such photonic quantum devices are huge. They often rely on the capacity to achieve adaptive measurement-based operations [ 43 , 44 ], and to have access to a large number of modes and initial coherent photons. In the meantime, sub-universal models have been proposed to achieve near-term quantum advantage. Those models are believed to have an intermediate computational advantage even without being able to achieve every operation that a fault-tolerant quantum computer could do. Boson Sampling [ 45 ], Gaussian Boson Sampling [ 46 ], or IQP circuits sampling [ 47 ] are good candidates, but the range of problems that one can solve using such approaches seems very limited. Finding an architecture able to offer a quantum utility to real life use case for quantum photonic device in the era of Noisy Intermediate-Scale Quantum [ 6] devices is an important field of research. Previous work also used linear optic circuits with post-processing strategy to simulate universal quantum computing [ 48 ], at the cost of strongly increasing the running time of the produced algorithms. In the previous chapters, it was explained how theoretical guarantees can be found regarding the training of subspace preserving quantum algorithms. The focus is proposed to be on QML algorithms based on such theoretical guarantees, and that focus on polynomial advantage. Linear optical circuits are good candidates for those applications, thank to their native subspace preserving properties, and their large repetition rate. However, QML applications for photonic platforms require to go beyond linear optic architectures to increase the expressivity of the 

31 CHAPTER 4. PHOTONIC SUB-OPTIMAL MODELS 

resulting models, but that does not necessarily depend on whether the model is universal for quantum computation. Previous works came up with alternative solutions to incorporate non-linearity вҖ” an important element for neural network architectures вҖ” such as global measurements and classical activation functions between linear optical layers [ 49 ], physical non-linear blocs [ 50 ], or adaptive gates to perform learning tasks [ 51 ]. In this Chapter, a new scheme of adaptivity is presented that allows to increase the controllability and maintain the subspace preserving properties of the circuits. 

# 4.1 Linear Optical Quantum Circuits 

## 4.1.1 Structure of Linear Quantum Optics 

Linear-optical networks are considered with m modes and set of simple optical elements (beam-splitters and phase-shifters). In general, those circuit are used while considering input states made with n identical photons that pass through the modes and optical elements and then measured to determine their locations. Here, adaptive photon-number measurements are not considered. Each element of the linear-optical networks/circuits may either be regarded as fixed , or 

parameterized , meaning that the gate has a tunable parameter Оё вҲҲ [0, 2ПҖ] that can be freely varied, corresponding to a beam-splitterвҖҷs angle or a phase-shifterвҖҷs phase . If there are p

parameterized gates in the circuit, we denote by Оё вҲҲ Оҳ := [ 0, 2ПҖ]p the tuple of all the parameter values. Into the circuit are sent n (indistinguishable) photons in some pure quantum state. A pure quantum state of n photons is a normalized vector in the n-photon Fock space , which is the Hilbert space of all complex superpositions of the basis Fock states |sssвҹ© (for all sss = ( s1 . . . , sm) вҲҲ

Nm such that s1 + В· В· В· + sm = n). We denote the set of basis Fock states by ОҰm,n, of which there are dn := |ОҰm,n| =  m+n+1

> n

 many. The direct sum of all the n-photon Fock spaces is known as the Fock space , which is infinite-dimensional. We will use the notations |sss| = вҲ‘mi=1 si and 

sss! = вҲҸmi=1 si!. An arrangement of beam-splitters and phase-shifters over m modes specifies a given unitary 

W 1 вҲҲ SU (m)1, which dictates the evolution of a single photon in the circuit. Conversely, all uni-taries in SU (m) may be realized as some arrangement of beam-splitters and phase-shifters [ 52 ]. A single-photon unitary W 1 вҲҲ SU (m) determines an n-photon unitary W n вҲҲ SU (dn), through the so-called (n -photon) photonic homomorphism : W n := П•(W 1) (see Theorem 4.1). Since

(4.4) Both BS and RBS perform a Оё -planar rotation between state |01 вҹ© and |10 вҹ©, but those states are Fock states in the photonic case, and qubit states in the HW preserving case. However, BS and RBS are subspace-preserving gates as RBS gates preserve the HW and BS gates preserve the 34 4.2. LIMITED CONTROLLABILITY OF LINEAR QUANTUM OPTICS 

number of particles. As a result, one can express the equivalent unitary of both BS and RBS based quantum circuits as block-diagonal as explained in [ 12 ] and [ 13 ]. We illustrate those block diagonal equivalent unitary matrices in Figure 4.1 for m modes, and in chapter 3. In these figures, each block represents the equivalent unitary when considering a fixed HW k or a fixed number of particles k. For the photonic case, the number of particles is unbounded, and the equivalent unitary is of infinite dimension. Both gates have the same impact on the initial state 

|00 вҹ©, |10 вҹ©, and |01 вҹ© but they act differently on the state |11 вҹ©. In addition, other initial Fock state can be considered for the photonic BS that does not map to any state for the RBS. Consider two quantum circuits, the first is a photonic circuit of m modes, and the second is a m-qubit quantum circuit. When considering the subspace of HW 1 (unitary subspace) for the RBS and the subspace of a single particle for the BS, a BS applied between modes i and 

j in the first circuit, and a RBS applied between qubit i and j will have the same effect. This is the reason why one can easily adapt the convolutional layer presented in [ 14 ], as the tensor encoding described in Eq. (1) of the main text ensures that, for each register, there is only one particle. For larger subspaces, the equivalent unitary matrices will differ. First, the size of the subspace for m qubits and HW k corresponds to the number of bitstring of m bits and HW k and is  mk

, while the size of the subspace for m modes and k particles is  m+kвҲ’1

> k

. In addition, authors in [ 54 ] explain that the homomorphism П• describes the way in which second-quantization enforces the evolution of k indistinguishable bosons, given the unitary evolution for a single boson. This relationship and its impact on linear optical quantum circuit controllability are discussed in [ 13 ]. For HW preserving quantum circuits, a similar relationship between the block can exists. For example, when considering only a line connectivity, RBS acts as a Fermionic BeamSplitter gates and each block W k is the k-compound matrix of W 1 [35 ]. For a greater connectivity, such relationship disapears and even if all the blocs are highly correlated, each subspace can be perfectly controled and are not always determined by the first one [12].

Other figures of merit for the expressivity of quantum models exist, including the distance to a 2-design [ 19 ] that characterizes the distribution of the unitary matrices, or the Fourier expressivity [25, 17]. In this work, we focus on the notion of controllability of the output state of the quantum circuit, i.e., the number of independent directions it can locally explore in the space of density matrices. Recent works have highlighted the impact of the controllability of quantum circuits, espe-cially in QML, by e.g. studying the rank of the Quantum Fisher Information Matrix (QFIM) [ 41 ], or the dimension of the Dynamical Lie Algebra [ 24 , 28 , 26 ] generated by the Hamiltonians 36 4.2. LIMITED CONTROLLABILITY OF LINEAR QUANTUM OPTICS 

in the circuit. Measurement based techniques are introduced to increase the controllability of photonic circuits. As they are not always CPTP maps, the controllability is studied at the level of the output stateвҖҷs density matrix. Studying circuits made of linear optics and adaptivity parts requires to consider a tool to characterize the controllability that is not only defined for unitary transformations. Accordingly, a new measure of controllability of the output state of a parametrized quantum circuit is introduced, using its corresponding Jacobian rank. 

Definition 4.1 (Number of degrees of freedom of a state) . The number of degrees of freedom 

of an n-photon state ПҒ(Оё ) at a point Оё in the parameter space is defined as the rank of Jacobian matrix of the map ПҒ : Оҳ вҶ’ CdnГ—dn calculated at point Оё :

DoF (ПҒ(Оё )) = rank [JПҒ(Оё )] . (4.7)

Since the considered circuits may apply successively operations that are standard parameter-ized optical gates, fixed optical gates, and state injections, overall the map Оё 7 вҶ’ ПҒ(Оё ) that sends a parameter tuple to the final stateвҖҷs density matrix is analytic 2, and consequently, the number of degrees of freedom Оё 7 вҶ’ DoF (ПҒ(Оё )) is constant almost-everywhere on the parameter space, due to [ 40 , Prop. B.4] (a more precise writing would be exactly the same as [ 12 , Lemma 4], with rank of density matrixвҖҷs Jacobian in place of rank of the pure stateвҖҷs Quantum Fisher Information Matrix (QFIM)). 

Theorem 4.2 (Almost-constant property of number of degrees of freedom) . Let ПҒ(Оё ) be the density matrix of the output state of a linear optical circuit, with or without state injections. > 2A function is analytic if it is smooth and if it agrees locally with its Taylor series around each point in the domain. Sines, cosines, as well as matrix products, sums, and exponentials, are all analytic; and compositions of analytic functions are still analytic. Hence, all the maps Оё7вҶ’ПҒout (Оё)considered in this work вҖ” even those including state injections вҖ” are analytic, being only compositions of sine, cosines, and products and sums of matrices.

The practical consequence of Theorem 4.2 is that drawing a point Оё вҲҲ Оҳ uniformly at random and calculating the stateвҖҷs number of degrees of freedom at that point yields DoF (ПҒ(Оё )) = DoF max (ПҒ) with probability 1. Hence in this work, we numerically evaluate the quantity DoF max (ПҒ) as follows: given an optical circuit over m modes and an n-photon input state ПҒin , we classically simulate the 

dn Г— dn output density matrix ПҒout (Оё ) through successive applications of beam-splitters and phase-shifters unitary channels and state injection CPTP maps, using PythonвҖҷs library PyTorch 

[ 57 ], which, by relying on automatic differentiation, enables us to access the Jacobian JПҒout (Оё )

of the output state. Then, we draw uniformly at random Оё , and calculate rank [JПҒ(Оё )] . With probability 1, this number is equal to DoF max (ПҒ) (Equation (4.8)). Since the controllability of the output state ПҒout (Оё ) lies entirely in the controllability of the single-photon unitary W 1(Оё ) of the whole circuit, we immediately have the following limitation. 

Theorem 4.3 (Controllability limitation of linear quantum optics) . Consider an n -photon pure state ПҒin := |ПҲin вҹ©вҹЁ ПҲin | entering an m-mode linear optical circuit W 1(Оё ) with p parametrized gates, and without state injections. Then, the controllability of the output density matrix 

ПҒout (Оё ) := W n(Оё )ПҒin W n(Оё )вҖ  is bounded by 

DoF max (ПҒout ) вүӨ dim ({W n(Оё ) | Оё вҲҲ Оҳ}) вүӨ m2 вҲ’ 1. (4.9) In fact, note that in the case where the input state consists of all photons in one same mode, i.e., ПҒin = |n, 0, . . . , 0вҹ©вҹЁ n, 0, . . . , 0|, an even tighter bound of O(m) instead of O(m2) may be shown to hold. 3

There exists subspace preserving quantum circuits that do not suffer from such controlla-bility limitations, e.g., RBS-based Hamming Weight preserving quantum circuits [ 12 ]. Those ansatz are particularly useful as they are likely to avoid vanishing gradient phenomena, the so-called barren plateau [10 ], when considering subspaces of polynomial dimension with respect to the number of qubits [ 24 , 28 , 26 ]. They can often be classically simulated [ 58 , 27 ], meaning that such quantum circuits would offer only polynomial advantage, if they are to offer any advantage at all. However, recent works have proposed quantum machine learning algorithms based on such ansatz [ 31 , 14 , 30 , 35 ]. In the following, we explain how SI can increase the controllability of the quantum circuit while maintaining вҖ“ if needed вҖ“ the subspace preserving properties of the circuit. 

> 3This bound can be obtained by an explicit calculation of the rank of the action of the derivative of the photonic homomorphism onto this initial state.

38 4.3. ADAPTIVITY SCHEMES 

# 4.3 Adaptivity Schemes 

## 4.3.1 Adaptive Linear Optic and State Injection Schemes 

In this Section, the State Injection (SI) scheme is introduced. This method allows to increase the expressivity of the photonic, and to perform tasks that are believed hard to do classically with fewer experimental constraints in comparison with the Adaptive Linear Optics (ALO) scheme [ 51 ], as explained in Section 4.3.3. In the following, the SI scheme is defined in Section 4.3.1.1 and its experimental implementation is motivated in Section 4.3.1.2. 

4.3.1.1 Scheme Definition 

The ALO scheme, that we illustrate in Figure 4.4b, was proposed by [ 51 ] where the authors built a feed-forward scheme for linear optical quantum computation. Their setup is composed of an input Fock state, with n photons spread across m modes, and k adaptive measurements. An adaptive measurement consists in measuring one mode using photon number resolving detector and configure the following (m вҲ’ 1) Г— (m вҲ’ 1) unitary according to the measurement result. 

Fig. 4.3: Quantum circuit made of linear optical blocks separated by state injections. We illustrate the SI scheme in Figure 4.3. It differs from the ALO scheme by the following. Firstly, no real time reconfiguration of the quantum circuit is required to be done within the run of the experiment as all unitary matrices are preset for each run. Secondly, our adaptive part comes from choosing what is the new Fock state to be injected in the circuit after a measurement. Lastly, the unitary does not shrink in size as the number of modes is preserved throughout the computation. 

Definition 4.2 (State Injection) . We call State Injection (SI) any operation on an m -mode photonic platform that performs photon-counting measurements in one or several modes, and, depending on the outcomes obtained, re-injects some photons back in one or several modes. Overall (since no single outcome is post-selected on), this operation is described by a CPTP map on the relevant Hilbert space. Different SI operations hence correspond to different choices 

39 CHAPTER 4. PHOTONIC SUB-OPTIMAL MODELS 

of modes that undergo measurements and/or re-injections, and different choices of rules that map measurement outcomes to the corresponding re-injections that should be performed. We refer to the latter as a choice of injection functions .

In the special case where the same k modes (here written as adjacent modes for simplic-ity) are being subject to both measurements and re-injections, and the total photon count is preserved, SI operations may be detailed as follows. If the photon count outcomes obtained when measuring those modes are n1, . . . , nk, then the re-injection process consists of injecting, 

in those modes only , the new state | f1(n1, . . . , nk), В· В· В· , fk(n1, . . . , nk)вҹ©. The injection functions are maps of the form fi : [[ 0, n]] k вҶ’ [[ 0, n]] k, where [[ a, b]] denotes the set of integers between two nonnegative integers a and b. Such functions may be chosen arbitrarily among those that respect the photon-number conservation constraint 

f1(n1, . . . , nk) + В· В· В· + fk(n1, . . . , nk) = n1 + В· В· В· + nk . (4.10) Preserving the number of particles is particularly interesting as it allows to perform subspace preserving computation. Recent works have shown theoretical guarantees on the training of such variational circuits on qubits [ 26 , 28 , 12 ]. Therefore, to ensure such theoretical guarantees, the subspace must be of size polynomial in the number of modes, imposing a constant number of initial photons. SI is a new tool that allows to go beyond the standard linear quantum optical circuits such as those used in Boson Sampling schemes. The diversity of possible encoding functions and measurement operators offers significant flexibility in the design of quantum models, but we will mainly focus, in this paper, on the case where we count the photons in one mode and inject the same number of photons. This choice is motivated by experimental consideration (clarified in Section 4.3.1.2), and by the preservation of the subspaces defined by a fixed number of particles. We use this example in Section 4.3.2.2, and in Section 4.3.3. In Section 4.3.1.2 we start by analyzing the general experimental framework that is common for both scheme, building on that we will highlight the differences between the ALO and SI schemes. It is important to keep in mind that the challenges of any scheme depend on the exact task and corresponding quantum circuit. In Section 4.3.3, we give an instance of a learning problem where the SI scheme can require less resources. In a more "near-term" perspective, one can adapt the schemes to implement a sub-universal model that matches the experimental capacity of a platform. For example, one can choose injection functions or the adaptive function in the feed-forward scheme to not depend on the measured number of photons to avoid photon counting. 40 4.3. ADAPTIVITY SCHEMES Photon Counting: 

> Delay
> State Injection

(a) State Injection channel for a single mode mea-sured. The purple SWAPs in the state injection part represent switches. The symbol f (p) rep-resents the adaptivity function that connects the measurement outcome p and the injected state. Photon Counting:
